<div id="review_1542721218" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1542721218" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1542721218?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=modern-classics">modern-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=borrowed-from-library">borrowed-from-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1542721218" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4300214076452462024">I finished this book last week and have been traveling through its landscape ever since, much like Sylvie rode her railcars from town to town. Marilynne Robinson creates characters that beg you to live with them, to dig deep and touch their souls. They are unlike any people you have ever known, and yet they are every person you have ever met. They struggle with how to connect to one another and how to suffer the loneliness of the connections they cannot make. The worlds that are most real are th</span>
  <span id="freeText4300214076452462024" style="display:none">I finished this book last week and have been traveling through its landscape ever since, much like Sylvie rode her railcars from town to town. Marilynne Robinson creates characters that beg you to live with them, to dig deep and touch their souls. They are unlike any people you have ever known, and yet they are every person you have ever met. They struggle with how to connect to one another and how to suffer the loneliness of the connections they cannot make. The worlds that are most real are those within their own heads. <br /><br />How much of who we are is in our DNA? Lucille and Ruthie are bereft of their mother through her suicide, they are raised by their grandmother, left to their two bumbling great-aunts and finally left to somehow find their way with their off-key aunt, Sylvie. Their problems are the same, but their reactions are worlds apart. Lucille sees in Sylvie a foreign creature and Ruthie sees a reflection of herself. There is isolation, abandonment, estrangement, and yearning; the desire to be a part of something and the need to be free.<br /><br />The efforts Sylvie and Ruthie make to fit into the roles society declares they must fill are heartbreaking. There is a thread of sadness, melancholy and near helplessness that runs through the lilting prose. I found myself rooting for the underdogs and despairing of hope for them in the same breath.<br /><br />If Marilynne Robinson had written only this novel, she would have cemented her place among the great writers of her time. She did more than this, however, and the body of her work has awed me. She waited 25 years between writing this book and her next, Gilead. I am hoping she will gift us with a few more before she ends her career.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4300214076452462024" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText4965707765565225841" style="display:none">"Perhaps all unsheltered people are angry in their hearts, and would like to break the roof, spine, and ribs, and smash the windows and flood the floor and spindle the curtains and bloat the couch." (237)<br /><br />Floods. Moments of homecoming. Departures. Then Boredom. Languid Days. School Days. Insufferable cold. &amp; stasis.<br /><br />Like some passably modern take on "Little Women", it's filled to the brim with detailed reminiscences, though set in Washington State. Like a Jane Champion film; like some beloved 90s indie lost in a shelf. A film that would've made you intensely cold in the (mostly vacant!) movie theater if you'd watched it there...<br /><br />"Housekeeping" comes from this long tradition of literary accounts of very eccentric /antisocial/neurotic/depressive Aunts. It is a theme that has pervaded through lit history (from Great Expectations to Cider With Rosie, The Romantics, heck, even in Perks of Being a Wallflower!). It works well. But, like the protagonist in this very solid coming-of-age IDYLL, we too want, anticipate, covet for something to occur for once... for SOME great event to finally transpire...</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17522373098479863752">I picked up Housekeeping initially at an airport bookstore, but couldn't bring myself to pay the more than full price they were asking. It took me months to remember to order it, which I regret on several levels. Dark, oddly twisted, Robinson managed to suck me into this strange world. Housekeeping is a book that manages to haunt me still every time I see it on my shelf or think of it. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1459170379" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1459170379" />
    <a title="·Karen·" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1652898-karen"><img alt="·Karen·" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434742215p2/1652898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1459170379?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="·Karen·" class="user" itemprop="url" name="·Karen·" href="/user/show/1652898-karen">·Karen·</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1459170379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer431147831034827051"><b>Look at that. And it’s not Versailles. It’s a brick wall with a ray of sunlight falling on it.</b><br/><br/>A summary of Marilynne Robinson's aesthetic in <a href='http://www.theparisreview.org/interviews/5863/the-art-of-fiction-no-198-marilynne-robinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Paris Review</a> emphasises the ability of an artist to make us view the quotidian with a sense of wonder. It's what she does, it's what her characters experience, it imbues them and us with a sense of the numinous in everyday life. <blockquote>
  <i>One evening one summer she went out to the garden. The earth in the rows was light and soft as cinders, pale clay yellow, a</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText431147831034827051" style="display:none"><b>Look at that. And it’s not Versailles. It’s a brick wall with a ray of sunlight falling on it.</b><br /><br />A summary of Marilynne Robinson's aesthetic in <a href="http://www.theparisreview.org/interviews/5863/the-art-of-fiction-no-198-marilynne-robinson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Paris Review</a> emphasises the ability of an artist to make us view the quotidian with a sense of wonder. It's what she does, it's what her characters experience, it imbues them and us with a sense of the numinous in everyday life. <blockquote>
  <i>One evening one summer she went out to the garden. The earth in the rows was light and soft as cinders, pale clay yellow, and the trees and plants were ripe, ordinary green and full of comfortable rustlings. And above the pale earth and bright trees the sky was the dark blue of ashes. As she knelt in the rows she heard the hollyhocks thump against the shed wall. She felt the hair lifted from her neck by a swift, watery wind, and she saw the trees fill with wind and heard their trunks creak like masts. She burrowed her hand under a potato plant and felt gingerly for the new potatoes in their dry net of roots, smooth as eggs. She put them in her apron and walked back to the house thinking What have I seen, what have I seen. The earth and the sky and the garden, not as they always are. And she saw her daughters' faces not as they always were, or as other people's were, and she was quiet and aloof and watchful, not to startle the strangeness away. She had never taught them to be kind to her.</i>
</blockquote><br />I daresay there are people who will be allergic to this kind of writing, probably finding it dense and over-precious, but those will have deserted this book by page 19 anyway, in search of something with more energy and a touch of excitement. A plot of some kind. No? <br />No. <br />Stay with this one. Take it slow. Savour those magisterial cadences, that weighty portentous movement from earth to sky to wind and back to earth, to new life coming from the earth in potatoes smooth as eggs. Into water, the formless element that flows and covers and washes and seeps, the female element, the ominous lake that shimmers and thunders and groans, the flood waters that simmer and brim and absorb the contours of the world into swirling green-grey. <br /><br />Those contours, the boundaries, they are essential here. <blockquote>
  <i>It was so dark that creatures came down to the water within a few feet of us. We could not see what they were. Lucille began to throw stones at them. "They're supposed to be able to smell us," she grumbled. For a while she sang "Mockingbird Hill," and then she sat down beside me in our ruined stronghold, never still, never accepting that all our human boundaries were overrun.<br />Lucille would tell this story differently. She would say I fell asleep, but I did not. I simply let the darkness in the sky become coextensive with the darkness in my skull and bowels and bones.</i>
</blockquote> Sylvie, too, loves the dark, loves sitting in the dark. There is a recurring image of the kind of isolation entailed in sitting in a lighted room or railway carriage at night. Windows offer not a view of the outside world, but a reflection of oneself, staring back. Those who are outside, at one with the dark, can see in, but those inside are blind to everything but that reflection of themselves. <br /><blockquote>
  <i>"One thing we could do," Sylvie said. Her voice was low and exulting.<br />"What?"<br />"Cross the bridge."<br />"Walk."<br />"Dogs wouldn't dare follow, and nobody'd believe them anyway. Nobody's ever done that. Crossed the bridge. Not that anybody knows of."<br />Well.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Well.<br /><br />Emerson: "Nothing is at last sacred but the integrity of your own mind." <br />Sylvie and Ruth achieve that integrity by inhabiting each other, by crossing the bridge, by becoming one with each other and the elements and nature and life and all humanity. Lucille is the one who cannot accept that her boundaries are overrun. She battles to assert her individuality through her clothes, her hair, her demarcation of difference within norms, her obsession with seeing herself as others will, standing outside herself and looking in. <br />Integrity: the state of being whole and undivided. <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="431147831034827051" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3617093825" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3617093825" />
    <a title="Pedro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73200741-pedro"><img alt="Pedro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599994103p2/73200741.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3617093825?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pedro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pedro" href="/user/show/73200741-pedro">Pedro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781250060655" href="/book/show/20696022-housekeeping">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73200741-pedro?shelf=buddy-reads">buddy-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73200741-pedro?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3617093825" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17093968714482864763">This novel is probably one of the best examples I have come across in recent years on how to tell a tragic story without a single drop of melodrama. It should actually be taken as an example by all those authors out there trying (too hard) to make their readers feel like they just peeled and chopped a bunch of onions.<br/><br/><i>Housekeeping</i> is a totally realistic story but because the writing takes it to a new level of brilliance I felt like it was all happening in a slightly different and much better worl</span>
  <span id="freeText17093968714482864763" style="display:none">This novel is probably one of the best examples I have come across in recent years on how to tell a tragic story without a single drop of melodrama. It should actually be taken as an example by all those authors out there trying (too hard) to make their readers feel like they just peeled and chopped a bunch of onions.<br /><br /><i>Housekeeping</i> is a totally realistic story but because the writing takes it to a new level of brilliance I felt like it was all happening in a slightly different and much better world than ours; A world of subtlety. <br /><br />Stunning writing, amazing characterisation and a haunting ending. <br /><br />What’s here not to love?<br /><br />A new (melancholy) favourite. <br /><br /><i>We are drifters. And once you have set your foot in that path it is hard to imagine another one.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="17093968714482864763" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_409368396" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/409368396" />
    <a title="Janice" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/229024-janice"><img alt="Janice" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368587278p2/229024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/409368396?book_show_action=true">Sep 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janice" href="/user/show/229024-janice">Janice</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/229024-janice?shelf=this-recording">this-recording</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/229024-janice?shelf=nys-writer-s-institute">nys-writer-s-institute</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer409368396" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7761402810655503922">Until recently, I thought I would like to one day live in a hotel. Not a cheap, seedy places with the lingering smell of stale cigarette smoke where people go to have affairs, or not one of those ultra sleek and modern trendy boutique hotels, where they sell “sensual massage kits” with the minibar items, but one of those classically glamorous places, with a piano bar, that one’s grandparents would stay in, like the Waldorf Astoria or the Carlyle in New York. (Also, the fact that I’ve never found</span>
  <span id="freeText7761402810655503922" style="display:none">Until recently, I thought I would like to one day live in a hotel. Not a cheap, seedy places with the lingering smell of stale cigarette smoke where people go to have affairs, or not one of those ultra sleek and modern trendy boutique hotels, where they sell “sensual massage kits” with the minibar items, but one of those classically glamorous places, with a piano bar, that one’s grandparents would stay in, like the Waldorf Astoria or the Carlyle in New York. (Also, the fact that I’ve never found drug paraphernalia on the window sill at the Waldorf, is a plus.) But, after spending quite a bit of time in hotels in the past few years, I’ve found that they start to get to me after a while. They’re beautiful of course, but they’re cold and empty and impersonal. And as hard as I try to keep the room neat with the addition of my personal belongings, their presence seems foreign and out of place, and when contrasted with the plush surroundings, seeing my stuff in such perfect environs, makes the room seem squalid.<br /><br />I found this book emulated the experience of staying in a hotel for too long. <i>Housekeeping</i> is about a doomed family. After the death of their grandmother, a pair of sisters is thrust into the care of their mentally unstable, transient aunt, that has an unfortunate penchant for hopping on freight trains and falling asleep on park benches. To me, generally, the plot is not really important. My focus is usually on the point source -- the means used to transmit a feeling or some sort of meaning. There was nothing about the conveyance of Robinson’s words that drew me in. I guess the kind of emotional blankness of the writing is supposed to act as a simulacrum of the remoteness of the main characters. (Duh, the book <i>is</i> told in the first person.) But, it was easy to forget the book was a first person narrative. There was such little insight into Ruthie’s internal life, that she became a complete non-entity. And maybe this was the point, but the intensity of her aloofness (and by her, I mean both Ruthie and Robinson), failed to impel any enthusiasm on my end. <br /><br />The prose was often beautiful, even poetic -- but it left me cold. Sometimes there was so much literary showboating, that it often felt like I was reading an MFA thesis project. To wit, <i>So shoes are worn and hassocks are sat upon and finally everything is left where it was and the spirit passed on, just as the wind in the orchard picks up the leaves from the ground as if there were no pleasure in the world but brown leaves, as if it would deck, clothe, flesh itself in flourishes of dusty brown apple leaves and then drop them all in a heap at the side of the house and goes on.</i> Other times, I felt as if this novel was perhaps written with the aid of one of those magnetic poetry kits: <i>So Fingerbone, or such relics of it as showed above the mirroring waters, seemed fragments of the quotidian held up to our wondering attention, offered somehow as proof of their own significance.</i> This felt like words that were strung together, because they “sounded pretty,” or seemed “deep,” but for me, they failed to evoke any emotion or impression of profundity. <br /><br />At some point while I was reading this, I thought “OK, this might be a three star book.” Despite my complaints, this book does have some positive attributes, (the aesthetics of the prose, chiefly). But then I stopped caring. And then I didn’t feel like reading it anymore. And then I just wanted to finish it, just so it would be over. And then I started to hate it. So, the process of writing this review, coupled with the resentment I accrued towards the second half when I no longer wanted to read it, has convinced me to rate at it two, which is consistent with my preference for being parsimonious with my affection. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7761402810655503922" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2223516480" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2223516480?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maxwell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maxwell" href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell">Maxwell</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2223516480" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer746333177942766118">This book's biggest strength is its sense of place. Robinson develops settings so well—all the way from state down to city, home, and the internal monologue of our narrator, Ruth. It's a very atmospheric novel that swept me away. However, I found the story to be a bit lacking. The novel ponders themes of isolation, home life, transience and familial relationships, but doesn't necessarily deliver a strong verdict on any of these things. That's ok; I don't expect to be hit over the head with what </span>
  <span id="freeText746333177942766118" style="display:none">This book's biggest strength is its sense of place. Robinson develops settings so well—all the way from state down to city, home, and the internal monologue of our narrator, Ruth. It's a very atmospheric novel that swept me away. However, I found the story to be a bit lacking. The novel ponders themes of isolation, home life, transience and familial relationships, but doesn't necessarily deliver a strong verdict on any of these things. That's ok; I don't expect to be hit over the head with what I should be thinking, especially while reading fiction. But Ruth is so naive and self-conscious and unsure of everything in life, which makes her a great observer but a rather dull narrator. Her Aunt Sylvie was far more fascinating, and I almost wish we had read from her perspective, or switched to hers halfway through. <b>3.5 stars.</b></span>

<div id="review_1348657707" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1348657707" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1348657707?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=rth-lifetime">rth-lifetime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=novel-a-biography">novel-a-biography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1348657707" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13288463692730195">Marilynne Robinson shrugged and thought "Maybe I'll write a book" and then just did it, in longhand, and then she showed it to her friends who lost their minds, and one of them was an author whose agent pounced on it and she got a call, like, "This is brilliant, get ready to be famous," and she was like "Oh, okay." <blockquote>The deep woods are as dark and stiff and as full of their own odors as the parlor of an old house. We would walk among those great legs, hearing the enthralled and incessant murmuring</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText13288463692730195" style="display:none">Marilynne Robinson shrugged and thought "Maybe I'll write a book" and then just did it, in longhand, and then she showed it to her friends who lost their minds, and one of them was an author whose agent pounced on it and she got a call, like, "This is brilliant, get ready to be famous," and she was like "Oh, okay." <blockquote>The deep woods are as dark and stiff and as full of their own odors as the parlor of an old house. We would walk among those great legs, hearing the enthralled and incessant murmurings far above our heads, like children at a funeral.</blockquote>I wonder how many struggling would-be novelists have read those sentences and just given up. Hopefully enough. “Here’s a first novel that sounds as if the author has been treasuring it up all her life, waiting for it to form itself,” <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/1981/01/07/books/books-of-the-times-books-of-the-times.html" title="NY Times review from 1981" rel="nofollow noopener">said the Times</a> instantly. It was nominated for the Pulitzer. It was 25 years before Robinson felt like writing another book.<br /><br />She's compared to Melville. "I thought that if I could write a book that had only female characters that men understood and liked, then I had every right to like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/199093132" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Moby-Dick,"</a> she says, and it's hard to find a piece about Robinson that doesn't mention Melville too. This is not because of her symbolism; although housekeeping is a symbol here, it doesn't have the smashing originality or unsubtlety that the Whale does. It's because it's about faith, which is alive and vital to Robinson the way it is to Melville. People who care this urgently are apt to sound radical about it:<blockquote>In the newness of the world God was a young man, and grew indignant over the slightest things. In the newness of the world God had perhaps not Himself realized the ramifications of certain of His laws, for example, that shock will spend itself in waves; that our images will mimic every gesture, and that shattered they will multiply and mimic every gesture ten, a hundred, or a thousand times.</blockquote>This isn't dogma; it's actual God, from a person who believes that the Bible is an actual thing. "It must mean something," Robinson says, "and I'm going to find out what." I'm an atheist, so I think the first statement is false, but I find her efforts awe-inspiring.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13288463692730195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11042662816580901671">Every episode represented a world of longing, so utterly beautifull. Longing to be like the others (Lucille), longing to being loved (Ruthie) and longing to a world of transcience and dwelling (Sylvie). On some places the longings meet each other while sometimes they collide. But the result is a magnificent novel about major feelings in one's life, all told in this slow, 'perceptive', strong language.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1041141079" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1041141079?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=500-wm">500-wm</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2015">r-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=prose-prose-prose">prose-prose-prose</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1041141079" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2073177794611835074"><blockquote>
  <i>When she remembered that we were there and that we were children she sometimes tried to make her stories useful.</i>
</blockquote>This is a highly regarded and much accredited piece, judging by the lists and the prizes and the place among the few females on many a personal favorite and/or grudgingly obliged pedestal. This is also a target of the Emperor's Clothing logos, judging from the MFA appraisals and sentence length critiques and the usual waving about a book like a dog worrying at tissue and string. I'</span>
  <span id="freeText2073177794611835074" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>When she remembered that we were there and that we were children she sometimes tried to make her stories useful.</i>
</blockquote>This is a highly regarded and much accredited piece, judging by the lists and the prizes and the place among the few females on many a personal favorite and/or grudgingly obliged pedestal. This is also a target of the Emperor's Clothing logos, judging from the MFA appraisals and sentence length critiques and the usual waving about a book like a dog worrying at tissue and string. I'll take what I can get of either and then some, but for godssakes spare me your "women's prose" or any similar breed of mewling and puking indoctrinated filth. The rhythm I felt was of Cormac McCarthy in his manner of naming to defining to carrying off into the maelstrom of cohesive myth, and if it is the lack of violence that weakens the association to many, for shame. I've no time for literary differentiation that won't keep its testosterone off the table.<blockquote>
  <i>So the transients wandered through Fingerbone like ghosts, terrifying as ghosts are because they were not very different from us.</i>
</blockquote>One piece of evidence of this work's "modern classic" status is how I have returned to my old style of reviewing, quotes and Docs and page numbers acknowledging bubbles of thought that cannot be explicated thoroughly enough by the echo chambers of my brain. It's not a distinction of quality but an indicator of difference, as my appreciation here is more academic than self-retrieved and thus requires the comfort of Ye Olde Microsoft, wherein I first came across the concept of habitus. The work is more cold and painstaking than reviews spun out of purer mental consent, making up for this lack with clear and piercing vistas that will eventually provide me with a living wage. Cynical, perhaps, but I can analyze accordingly and speak the vernacular well enough, so don't mind how I expand habitus on lines of landscape, human, dream, and the cleaving by institution of those who fail. Dust to dust, water to water, illustrated most deeply by those who never learn to fear its handfuls.<blockquote>
  <i>...just when I had got used to the limits and dimensions of one moment, I was expelled into the next and made to wonder again if any shapes hid in its shadows. That most moments were substantially the same did not detract at all from the possibility that the next moment might be utterly different. And so the ordinary demanded unblinking attention. Any tedious hour might be the last of its kind.</i>
</blockquote>On the one hand, this tempo of prose (it matches my breathbound pulse of vocalized memory well enough, so I am on the side of the favoring) pleases with Proustian connotations and the swell of burgeoning thought. On the other, this all too familiar body hatred and romanticizing of poverty (it is impossible to gloss over the menstruation cycle in the life of the homeless) cannot be made up for with the hasty scramble to establish a society and all its ills within the last twenty pages. It does not surprise me that it is on all the lists because it offers a tidbit of novel while following all the rules. It does not sadden me that it is on all the lists because it afforded me a glimpse of the House of Usher, ill remembered and thus more freely applied to visions of foundering and all the stigma of its provocateurs. Civilization forbids the habitus of suicide, survival, and self-sufficiency, leastwise without the proper compensation. They never would have tried to take the child away had the family owned the lake.<blockquote>
  <i>“What are you doing?” Lucille asked.<br />“This dictionary is full of pressed flowers,” I said.<br />“Grandpa.”<br />“He put lady’s slippers under <b>O</b>. Probably <b>orchids</b>.”<br />“Let me see that,” Lucille said. She took the book by each end of its spine and shook it. Scores of flowers and petals fell and drifted from between the pages. Lucille kept shaking until nothing more came, and then she handed the dictionary back to me. “Pinking shears,” she said.<br />“What will we do with these flowers?”<br />“Put them in the stove.”<br />“Why do that?”<br />“What are they good for?”</i>
</blockquote>This all takes place in Idaho, home of a singly visited vacation house and some of the highest rates of hate crime in the states. Food for thought, if nothing else.<blockquote>
  <i>…just as the wind in the orchard picks up the leaves from the ground as if there were no other pleasure in the world but brown leaves, as if it would deck, clothe, flesh itself in flourishes of dusty brown apple leaves, and then drops them all in a heap at the side of the house and goes on.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="2073177794611835074" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer190551513" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer3216828233632381312">Lyrical. Beautiful. Haunting.<br/><br/>There were so many scenes in this book that reminded me a little of my own childhood or that made me wish I could have experienced them back then, but then there was a sadness to it that never ended.<br/><br/> Still, how neat to take a boat and spend the night in the woods and hop a train to get home. Of course, the boat was stolen. Or the ice skating: I have never read anything so beautiful as the way Marilynne Robinson described it. And then how their mother had sung "What'l</span>
  <span id="freeText3216828233632381312" style="display:none">Lyrical. Beautiful. Haunting.<br /><br />There were so many scenes in this book that reminded me a little of my own childhood or that made me wish I could have experienced them back then, but then there was a sadness to it that never ended.<br /><br /> Still, how neat to take a boat and spend the night in the woods and hop a train to get home. Of course, the boat was stolen. Or the ice skating: I have never read anything so beautiful as the way Marilynne Robinson described it. And then how their mother had sung "What'll I Do?, which was also a favorite of my own mother, who also used to sing it.<br /><br />There was just so much to this book and yet it was still a short read. It should be made into a movie. Later. They did make a movie out of it. I bought it on Amazon, and it was really good.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1788441571" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1788441571" />
    <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes"><img alt="aPriL does feral sometimes " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478243665p2/3409532.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1788441571?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="user" itemprop="url" name="aPriL does feral sometimes " href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes">aPriL does feral sometimes </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6305677-housekeeping">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=unbearably-sad">unbearably-sad</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=brilliant-but-i-don-t-love-it">brilliant-but-i-don-t-love-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=illuminating">illuminating</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1788441571" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer184344770174100422">I can recommend 'Housekeeping' only to readers of experimental literature and to budding new writers and students of MFA programs. And to curious wilders and primitive self-taught readers of literature like me. It is a book of gloriously lyrical language, dense with description and atmosphere and no real action. Days pass, seasons pass, but time has stopped, nonetheless.<br/><br/>Water and dead tree leaves are among the things at effortless rest in the world, almost like dandelion seeds - but seeds are al</span>
  <span id="freeText184344770174100422" style="display:none">I can recommend 'Housekeeping' only to readers of experimental literature and to budding new writers and students of MFA programs. And to curious wilders and primitive self-taught readers of literature like me. It is a book of gloriously lyrical language, dense with description and atmosphere and no real action. Days pass, seasons pass, but time has stopped, nonetheless.<br /><br />Water and dead tree leaves are among the things at effortless rest in the world, almost like dandelion seeds - but seeds are alive, full of potential fecundity. Not so are dead leaves. Movement is a result of being pushed by natural forces, primarily the wind. These things drift mindlessly from one moment to another, not choosing, being in the moment, relaxing into the forces, rippling, swaying without internal energy, without outward resistance. Marilynne Robinson fills her unique story with these images. <br /><br />Some readers think the story is about being different, non-conforming. I don't.<br /><br />'Housekeeping' is a high concept art novel. Instead of drawing with lines or painting with splashes of paint or sculpting out a form from a material, Marilynne Robinson has used words and descriptions to draw, paint and sculpt out a literary shape of two drifting characters, unresisting beings in human form, lives lived like a leaf floating on water. Or drowning. Without actually dying.<br /><br />I was reminded a bit of Chauncey Gardiner in '<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/677877.Being_There" title="Being There by Jerzy Kosiński" rel="nofollow noopener">Being There</a>' by Jerzy Kosiński. Life is experienced as something which happens, but unlike Chauncey, for Sylvie Fisher there is very little impetus from the outside world as well as from within which actually compels her, life being lived more like the accidental bumping of stable, unreactive atoms. <br /><br />Ruth, the narrator, ruminates and remembers like someone in a sensory deprivation tank, remembering the past (fortunately for readers, in a coherent time-stream of forward storytelling). The little girl Ruth and thirty-five year old Aunt Sylvie, the main protagonists, choose to live like 'noble' elements, but since they still have flesh, their brains still snap with synaptic charge. <br /><br />Ruth thinks and remembers and thinks and thinks in almost a stream of flashback consciousness, but with punctuation and poetic lyricism, for over 200 pages. She thinks of her younger sister, Lucille; of her mother, Helen, dead by suicide; of her great-aunts, Misses Lily and Nona Foster who used to live like moles in an underground house and who now live as permanent transients in a hotel; of Mrs. Sylvia Foster of Fingerbone, Idaho, her grandmother; and of especially her aunt, Mrs. Sylvia Fisher (who eventually caught Ruth in her net). <br /><br />Ruth thinks over and over about the historical stories of her relatives, especially the story of her grandfather, Edmund Foster, who died riding in a train which fell off a local Fingerbone bridge into a nearby lake, and Helen, her mother, who drowned after driving her car into the same still lake. She thinks about how they might live now as elements in the places where they died.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ She muses over every glass of water that she is drinking in her grandmother's house that particles of her mother and grandfather and all of the other people in the train are in it.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Ruth thinks deeply about these things, and other matters of her childhood experience, remembering and conjecturing as if her observations of her relatives were being done under a microscope, albeit with an imaginary examination of their lives only since that is all she has of them. Her thoughts are like a meandering creek, bubbling briefly over rocks, sliding a little rough over sand, but never looking beyond or forward for herself.<br /><br />The narration, a coming-of-age memoir, is as if Ruth were in a state of numb mental shock, but her mother Helen, her grandmother Sylvia, and her grandfather Edmund all died some time ago. <br /><br /><br />Ruth and Lucille are orphaned at a very young age. They were just starting elementary school when their mother, Helen, abandoned by her husband Reginald Stone (perhaps a traveling tool equipment salesman) inexplicably packs up the girls and leaves their Seattle apartment and returns to her mother's house in Fingerbone, Idaho. Helen abandons the girls on her mother's porch, then drives away. Witnesses tell of her determined suicide by trying to drive her car - twice - into the lake, which finally succeeds in her second attempt. <br /><br />After their grandmother Sylvia Foster dies, five years after the girls moved in with her, Ruth's great-aunts Lily and Nona come and stay a short while in the grandmother’s house. Then they leave quickly, returning to their hotel rooms in Spokane. They were uncomfortable in Sylvia's house and in trying to take care of two orphaned girls. They put an advertisement in newspapers before they leave, requesting Sylvia Fisher come home. Sylvia Fisher is the daughter of grandmother Sylvia Foster and the aunt of Ruth and Lucille. <br /><br />When Sylvie Fisher arrives, she seems normal enough. Lily and Nona know something of Sylvie's abnormal hobo behaviors, but they do not want to live in the small town Fingerbone and they can't take the girls to their apartment in the hotel which does not accept children. Besides, the semi-tamed mountainous forest which surrounds Fingerbone scares them. So Sylvie Fisher moves back into her aging childhood home, and begins the attempt to be a housekeeper. <br /><br />Lucille and Ruth, still young children, follow Sylvie's odd adult leadership. However, it soon becomes clear Sylvie either cannot or does not do parenting. Instead, she acts as if she is living in the world without a body or a need of possessions or physical comforts. She stands for hours without moving. She disappears into the nearby forest for an entire day. Breakfast and dinner is maybe marshmallows and graham crackers. Hair goes uncombed and clothes are worn for days, unwashed. The wonders of nature or the passage of clouds in the sky can occupy Sylvie literally for days. <br /><br />I think Sylvie is crazy, actually, but in a mostly non-dangerous way. However, I'd NEVER let her take charge of girls under 18 without supervision. She genuinely marches to a different drummer. I think Sylvie definitely has some brain damage issue. Ruth and Lucille are still grieving and frightened, plus they have no other options than having Sylvie parent them. <br /><br />The girls are scared and needy after their grandmother and mother are gone. The chaos of the Universe is attacking them - they are excruciatingly vulnerable. Sylvie is their only lifeline to survival. They both look desperately to her for their salvation. But what Sylvie teaches is lessons in self-abnegation, not engagement.<br /><br />Ruth's younger sister Lucille manages to pull herself forward, even after so many grievious losses, making friends and attending school in Fingerbone, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[moving into her home economics teacher's home, presumedly learning how to do housekeeping, appreciating the accumulation of household goods and ornaments - not to mention, presumably, marriage, children, occupation and hobbies, <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />Ruth takes a different path.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Lucille rejects Sylvie and her lifestyle. Lucille finds she does not have the mental or physical tolerance for Sylvie’s zen-buddhist nonsense, which is entirely outside the lines of normal thinking and desires. Lucille wants to escape the elemental chaos, loneliness and dangers of life. She wants the solid controllable security of functional housekeeping and herd conformity. She wants a solid sense of the ground beneath her feet and the order of process - the certainty of the sun rising every day in the same place. <br /><br />Ruth, who becomes a willing student of Sylvie's lessons in numbing the mind and body with starvation, freezing cold and other anesthetizing deprivations, is giddy and relieved to discover that living benumbed in the transitory moment without intense feelings or worry from not owning possessions or caring about the future, away from physical comfort, feels like flowing with and simultaneously like defeating the chaos, a non-being being. Ruth loves being an unnoticed minor current in the constant winds of any disaster which she knows is sure to come. It probably feels to Ruth like she has finally mastered the chaos of uncertainty and grief through the mechanism of endless directionless traveling. <br /><br />I think she hopes to avoid in the future any agonies of loss and fear. Ruth must want to avoid her poor grandfather's and mother's derailment. They both were knocked off sideways from the supposedly safe track of their jobs, marriages and homes. Ruth’s vagabond life will keep her permanently benumbed. Harsh cold weather, rarely eating, physical exhaustion and loneliness increases the forgetting. She will keep to an emotionally dulled, transient life of running from loss and fear forever.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I can't say I entirely enjoyed the novel. It is too provocative. And tempting, frankly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="184344770174100422" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3859301" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3859301" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/216284-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1254348059p2/216284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3859301?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/216284-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/216284-mark?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3859301" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11161200088561386796">Marilynne Robinson won great praise a couple years ago for "Gilead," and much was made of the fact that it had been 23 years since she had written her first novel, "Housekeeping." While this was an evocative tale about a family in an isolated rural area and the writing was often poetic, I found it a struggle to get through. Heavy on atmospherics and light on plot, it was the kind of book where I often found myself nodding off on mid-page. Not my cup of tea.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Li7lA/DCRgxnoAwWTjIUxVt75ryrFUc9nUfPVfccVAnkGSVQB8CEJgGDsZ3C6zYuJhRocwGUs3XA7xmAyctQug==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

In this absorbing exploration of Women\'s Aglow Fellowship, the largest women\'s evangelical organization in the world, R. Marie Griffith challenges the simple generalizations often made about charismatic or &quot;spirit-filled&quot; Christian women and uncovers important connections between Aglow members and the feminists to whom they so often seem opposed. Women\'s Aglow is an international, interdenominational group of &quot;spirit-filled&quot; women who meet outside the formal church structure for healing prayer, worship, and testimony. Aglow represents a wider evangelical culture that has gained recent media attention as women inspired by the Christian men\'s group, Promise Keepers, have initiated parallel groups such as Praise Keepers and Promise Reapers. These groups are generally newcomers to an institutional landscape that Aglow has occupied for thirty years, but their beliefs and commitments are very similar to Aglow\'s. While historians have examined earlier women\'s prayer groups, they\'ve tended to ignore these modern-day evangelical groups because of their assumed connection to the &quot;religious right.&quot;God\'s Daughters reveals a devotional world in which oral and written testimonies recount the afflictions of human life and the means for seeking relief and divine assistance. A relationship with God, envisioned as father, husband or lover, and friend, is a way to come to terms with pain, dysfunctional family relationships, and a desire for intimacy. Griffith\'s book is also valuable in showing the complex role that women play within Pentecostalism, a movement that has become one of the most important in twentieth-century world As they confront an array of common catastrophes - a colicky baby, a faltering marriage, stalled ambitions - the wife analyzes her predicament, invoking everything from Keats and Kafka to the thought experiments of the Stoics to the lessons of doomed Russian cosmonauts. Thatcher\'s policies have put husbands and sons out of work, and the city\'s notorious drugs epidemic is waiting in the wings. Shuggie\'s mother Agnes walks a wayward path: she is Shuggie\'s guiding light but a burden for him and his siblings. She dreams of a house with its own front door while she flicks through the pages of the Freemans catalogue, ordering a little happiness on credit, anything to brighten up her grey life. Married to a philandering taxi-driver husband, Agnes keeps her pride by looking good--her beehive, make-up, and pearly-white false teeth offer a glamourous image of a Glaswegian Elizabeth Taylor. But under the surface, Agnes finds increasing solace in drink, and she drains away the lion\'s share of each week\'s benefits--all the family has to live on--on cans of extra-strong lager hidden in handbags and poured into tea mugs. Agnes\'s older children find their own ways to get a safe distance from their mother, abandoning Shuggie to care for her as she swings between alcoholic binges and sobriety. Shuggie is meanwhile struggling to somehow become the normal boy he desperately longs to be, but everyone has realized that he is &quot;no right,&quot; a boy with a secret that all but him can see. Agnes is supportive of her son, but her addiction has the power to eclipse everyone close to her--even her beloved Shuggie.\n\nA heartbreaking story of addiction, sexuality, and love, Shuggie Bain is an epic portrayal of a working-class family that is rarely seen in fiction. Recalling the work of Edouard Louis, Alan Hollinghurst, Frank McCourt, and Hanya Yanagihara, it is a blistering debut by a brilliant novelist who has a powerful and important story to tell.<\/span>\n           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1076927756640657953">Marilynne Summers Robinson (born November 26, 1943) is an American novelist and essayist. Across her writing career, Robinson has received numerous awards, including the Pulitzer Prize for Fiction in 2005, National Humanities Medal in 2012, and the 2016 Library of Congress Prize for American Fiction. In 2016, Robinson was named in Time magazine's list of 100 most influential people.[2] Robinson be</span>
  <span id="freeText1076927756640657953" style="display:none">Marilynne Summers Robinson (born November 26, 1943) is an American novelist and essayist. Across her writing career, Robinson has received numerous awards, including the Pulitzer Prize for Fiction in 2005, National Humanities Medal in 2012, and the 2016 Library of Congress Prize for American Fiction. In 2016, Robinson was named in Time magazine's list of 100 most influential people.[2] Robinson began teaching at the Iowa Writers' Workshop in 1991[3] and retired in the spring of 2016.[4]<br /><br />Robinson is best known for her novels Housekeeping (1980) and Gilead (2004). Her novels are noted for their thematic depiction of both rural life and faith.[5] The subjects of her essays have spanned numerous topics, including the relationship between religion and science, US history, nuclear pollution, John Calvin, and contemporary American politics.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1076927756640657953" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1056302-housekeeping">Quotes from Housekeeping</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;To crave and to have are as like as a thing and its shadow. For when does a berry break upon the tongue as sweetly as when one longs to taste it, and when is the taste refracted into so many hues and savors of ripeness and earth, and when do our senses know any thing so utterly as when we lack it? And here again is a foreshadowing -- the world will be made whole. For to wish for a hand on one's hair is all but to feel it. So whatever we may lose, very craving gives it back to us again.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/47882-to-crave-and-to-have-are-as-like-as-a">342 likes</a>
            </nobr>

          </div>
